## Payer2019_NatRevGenet_TE-Disease

```
Much of the human genome sequence is made up 
of interspersed repeats derived from the activity of 
transposable elements (TEs)1,2, mainly retrotransposons. 
Expression of retrotransposition-​competent TEs can 
lead to more insertions. Therefore, limiting TE expres-
sion is fundamental to genome integrity, as the insertion 
of new elements into the human genome can disrupt 
gene function or alter gene expression, contributing 
to disease.
Retrotransposition events can result in large sequence 
insertions. This process creates a distinct ‘brand’ of 
genetic variation that is qualitatively different from 
nucleotide substitutions and other mutagenic mecha-
nisms. That is, whereas mobile element insertion var-
iants can be likened to copy number variants (CNVs) 
with respect to size, TE insertions differ qualitatively 
from the typical CNV as a result of their sequence con-
tent. Although CNVs can contain regulatory or coding 
sequences, their sequence content is often random. 
By contrast, TE insertions often encompass functional 
sequences intrinsic to the mobile element. Depending on 
the type of TE, an insertion polymorphism can include 
a promoter for autonomous expression, other regula-
tory sequences that lead to heterochromatin formation, 
marked secondary RNA or DNA structures and splic-
ing regulators or protein-​coding sequences. Another 
major difference between a TE insertion and a large-​
scale duplication is the copy number; thousands of TE 
copies exist in the human genome, which provide ample 
templates for pairing of these sequences in DNA, result-
ing in recombination and further genomic rearrange-
ments, or in RNA, leading to altered splicing, editing 
or degradation.
The intrinsic regulatory potential of these sequences 
provides unique substrates for evolution. There are 
several possible outcomes for any retrotransposed 
sequence. The sequence can be lost from a population 
either by genetic drift or through negative selection in 
the case of deleterious insertions3. Insertion alleles that 
are better tolerated can become common variants or 
fixed alleles, largely through genetic drift, although posi­
tive selection may also occur4. Although TE sequences 
can introduce functions to loci, as transcriptional regu-
lators5–9 and splicing determinants10–12, these effects are 
often balanced by host regulatory factors that minimize 
the functional effects of TE insertions (Box 1). Over 
evolutionary time, TEs can lose, retain or modify these 
functions, which can lead to adaptive advantages, and 
ancient TEs have been repeatedly co-​opted for bene-
ficial gene regulatory ‘network wiring’ functions5 or 
‘scavenged’ for their protein-​coding capacity (Box 1). 
TEs can also affect genome function and organization 
by promoting intrachromosomal or interchromosomal 
rearrangements13. Hence, the same functions that make 
TEs useful on an evolutionarily scale can manifest in 
disease risk.
Because TEs exist in high copy numbers and share 
high sequence homology, particularly for the most 
recent insertions, these elements have historically been 
poorly incorporated in genome assemblies and under-​
ascertained in many surveys of genetic variation. The 
scale of variation, both de novo insertions and com-
mon polymorphisms, inherent to these repeats was first 
demonstrated by targeted amplification strategies14–17. 
Recent advances in short-​read sequence analysis and 
anticipated advances leveraging long-​read technol-
ogies are contributing to higher accuracy in map-
ping de novo TE insertions and more comprehensive 
catalogues of common variants18–20. These advances, 
coupled with increasingly comprehensive lists of mono-
genic disease genes as well as disease risk loci identi-
fied in genome-​wide association studies (GWAS), 
Transposable elements
(TEs). DNA sequences that 
have the ability to move 
(transpose) to new locations in 
the genome.
Retrotransposons
DNA sequences that 
proliferate in the genome using 
an RNA intermediate and a 
‘copy-​and-paste’ 
retrotransposition mechanism.
Transposable elements in human 
genetic disease
Lindsay M. Payer1 and Kathleen H. Burns   1,2*
Abstract | Transposable elements are abundant in the human genome, and great strides have 
been made in pinpointing variations in these repetitive sequences using whole-​genome 
sequencing. Now , the focus is shifting to understanding their expression and regulation, and  
the functional consequences of their insertion and retention in the genome over time. Whereas 
transposable element insertions have been known to cause human genetic disease since the 
1980s, the scope of their contributions to heritable phenotypes is now starting to be uncovered. 
Here, we review the many ways human retrotransposons contribute to genome function, their 
dysregulation in diseases including cancer and how they affect genetic disease.

Baltimore, MD, USA.
2McKusick–Nathans Institute 
of Genetic Medicine, Sidney 
Kimmel Comprehensive 
Cancer Center, Johns Hopkins 
University School of Medicine, 
Baltimore, MD, USA.
*e-​mail: kburns@jhmi.edu

have expanded the number of TEs we recognize as 
candidates for causing disease.
This Review discusses the multiple ways that TEs 
of different ages relate to human disease. Altered TE 
expression has been described in many malignancies 
and can lead to somatic retrotransposition. Germline 
insertions with overt effects on gene function can cause 
monogenic disease, and insertions with fewer functional 
effects that have become common variants in popu­
lations can contribute to the risk of complex diseases. 
TEs that are evolutionarily old and have become fixed 
in the human genome can serve as recombination 
substrates, whereby unequal crossovers can result in 
monogenic disease.

Box 1 | Repurposing transposable element sequences
Transposons develop and distribute new functional sequences that can 
add value to the host. In numerous instances, transposable elements 
(TEs) have provided regulatory sequences and protein-​coding domains 
that have been co-​opted to serve the purposes of the host.
Because retrotransposons rely on RNA intermediates for their 
propagation, recent insertions often contain transcriptional activators. 
Endogenous retroviruses (ERVs) encompass strong RNA polymerase II 
promoters and enhancers that can affect gene expression over considerable 
distances9 (see the figure, part a, red arrow). Meanwhile, host species keep 
mobile element expression in check by targeting TEs for epigenetic 
silencing. The most common genes in our genome encode the Krüppel-​
associated box (KRAB) domain-​containing zinc-​finger proteins (KZFPs), 
which seem to have evolved for this purpose49,50. KZFPs recruit KRAB-​
associated protein 1 (KAP1; also known as TRIM28)49,51,52, SETDB1 and DNA 
methyltransferases (DNMTs) that promote heterochromatin formation. 
Mobile elements are under selective pressure to escape silencing, and 
KZFPs co-​evolve to enforce silencing. The collateral in this arms race is 
the accumulation of variations of zinc finger binding proteins and the 
widespread distribution of their cognate binding motifs in the genome, 
which can be repurposed for gene regulation122. At any locus, a new 
retrotransposon insertion delivers a target with antithetical potentials:  
a trans-​activating function that is bound by repressive silencers (blue protein 
complex). Both properties may be lost over time (main arrow), but either 
may become entrenched if it serves a purpose (side arrows). Retroviral-​
derived sequences have been co-​opted to ‘wire’ gene networks, 
regulating genes important for the development of the placenta123,124 
and interferon-stimulated genes5,8.
TEs similarly introduce sequences with repressed potential at the 
RNA level (see the figure, part b). Alu insertions are perhaps the best 
examples of this. These repeats underwent a burst of expansion in 
the primate lineages and have accumulated in introns over time1. 
In preprocessed mRNAs, these sequences are bound by heterogeneous 
nuclear ribonucleoprotein C (hnRNP C; blue protein), which masks splicing 
sites intrinsic to the repeat (red)12. Over time, changes in the Alu sequence 
can disrupt hnRNP C binding and increase splice acceptor and splice 
donor site function, providing new alternative or constitutive exons to 
transcripts10,125,126. Although it is rare on a genome-​wide scale that 
constitutive, protein-​coding exons are derived from Alu sequences, nearly 
5% of alternatively spliced human exons are Alu-​derived125. Thus, Alu 
exonization has a notable effect on the human transcriptome.
In these scenarios, the host species gains adaptive advantages for living 
with TEs — it is easier to lose functions than create new ones.
Co-​option of TE-​encoded proteins
There are also instances of TE ‘domestication’ whereby a protein-​coding 
domain is taken from a mobile element to become a host gene. In some 
cases, such as RAG recombinase, this serves an essential purpose. RAG1/2 
are derived from a Transib DNA transposable element, wherein its 
‘cut-and-paste’ transposition mechanism has been repurposed for V(D)J 
recombination to diversify antigen-​binding receptors on lymphocytes127–132. 
Mutations of RAG genes cause immunodeficiency syndromes (OMIM 
233650, 601457, 603554). Several other DNA transposons have been 
co-opted in full or in part to give rise to genes such as the pogo-​derived 
centromere protein B (CENPB), and piggyBac and tigger-​derived genes133–135. 
Pogo transposable element derived with ZNF domain (POGZ) is important 
for neural development, and mutations in the gene cause White–Sutton 
syndrome (OMIM 616364)136,137. Also, piggyBac-​derived gene 5 (PGBD5) is 
notable because it has recently been proposed to function as an endogenous 
mutagen and potential therapeutic target in paediatric cancers138–140.
Retroelement domestication also occurs. Perhaps the most spectacular 
example are human ERV envelope (Env) proteins, syncytins, which have 
been co-​opted to function in cell–cell fusion and play a role in placenta 
formation141–143. Interestingly, syncytin genes have been identified in all 
placental species, with many independent co-​options of retroviral proteins 
in mammals — and even in viviparous lizards144. In all, the HUGO Gene 
Nomenclature Committee annotates 100 human genes as being part 
retrotransposon. Half of these genes encode SRE-​ZBP, CTfin51, AW-1 
and Number 18 cDNA (SCAN)-domain-​containing proteins. SCAN 
domains mediate protein–protein interactions, including homo- and 
hetero-​oligomerization, and are derived from a portion of the Gag capsid 
protein from Gypsy or Ty3-like retrotransposons145. Most human SCAN-​
domain-containing proteins are zinc finger proteins organized with the 
SCAN domain at the N terminus; a subset have intervening KRAB motifs 
and may have evolved to meet needs for retroelement silencing.
NaTuRE REvIEws | GEnETiCS
Reviews
	
 volume 20 | DECEMBER 2019 | 761
Retrotransposons in humans
Types of retrotransposons. In modern humans, ongo-
ing retrotransposon activity is driven by a single fam-
ily of protein-​coding (autonomous) retrotransposons, 
known as long interspersed element 1 (LINE-1 or L1). 
L1 sequences are found throughout eutherian mammals 
and make up 17% of the human genome1,2. Similarly, L1 
sequences are substantial constituents of other primate 
genomes21. Although there are hundreds of thousands 
of copies of L1 in the human genome, only ~100 are 
fully intact, and a handful are highly active or ‘hot’ L1 
(refs22,23). L1 sequences are ‘copy-​and-paste’ TEs or 
retrotransposons that accumulate in genomes using 
RNA intermediates. A full-​length L1 is 6 kb (Fig. 1a) and 
encodes an internal RNA polymerase II (Pol II) pro-
moter. When transcribed, L1 produces a bicistronic RNA 
that encodes the RNA-​binding protein ORF1p24,25 and a 
protein with endonuclease26,27 and reverse transcriptase28 
activities, ORF2p. Both are essential for L1 to replicate 
or retrotranspose, which is accomplished via a pro-
cess termed target primed reverse transcription (TPRT)29 
(Fig. 1b), whereby L1 reverse transcribes its RNA and 
integrates the cDNA into the genome. Most genomic 
L1 sequences are truncated at the 5′ end, reflecting 
defects in TPRT30 or perhaps competition between the 
completion of the retrotransposition reaction and host 
mechanisms that limit retrotransposition.
L1-encoded ORF2p has a so-​called ‘cis preference’ or 
tendency to retrotranspose the RNA that encoded it as 
opposed to RNA from another L1 (ref.31). Non-​coding 
retrotransposons have evolved that can effectively co-​
opt ORF2p. Alu sequences, short interspersed elements 
(SINEs) about 300 bp in length and transcribed by 
Pol III, have been the most proliferative retrotransposon 
in primates32–34 (Fig. 1a). Alu sequences constitute approx-
imately 11% of the human genome1. SVA elements, com-
posites of SINE, variable number tandem repeat and Alu 
sequences approximating 2 kb in size (Fig. 1a), are also 
mobilized in trans by ORF2p35. ORF2p can also reverse 
transcribe U6 RNAs, forming integrations of U6–3′-L1 
chimaeras36,37, and cellular mRNAs, forming integra-
tions of processed pseudogenes38. All of these elements 
are sources of genetic variation today.
Endogenous retroviruses (ERVs) (Fig. 1a) are active in 
other species, including mouse39, but seem to have been 
rendered nearly immobile in humans within the last few 
million years40,41. This loss of activity is recent enough 
that not all ERVs are fixed alleles, but rather insertion 
polymorphisms (that is, pre-​insertion or ‘empty’ alleles 
and insertion alleles) still segregate in human popula-
tions42,43. Human ERV (HERV) polymorphisms can also 
arise as deletion variants, leaving proviral and collapsed 
‘solo’ long terminal repeat (LTR) alleles44. Recently, a full-​
length and potentially infectious provirus was reported 
on Xq21.33 in a single individual43. This finding indi-
cates that active HERV alleles could persist in modern 
humans or be newly formed through the recombination 
of two defective proviruses40. ERV LTRs encode strong 
Pol II promoter activities and are frequent transcrip-
tional start sites for genes, chimeric transcripts and long 
non-​coding RNAs (lncRNAs)45,46.
TE activity generates genetic diversity. Most new TE inser-
tions are not detrimental to fitness and are not selected 
against. Thus, they may persist in a population, and 
genetic drift allows a subset of these to become common 
alleles. These mobile element insertions exist as polymor-
phisms — also referred to as retrotransposon insertion 
polymorphisms or polymorphic TEs — until some ulti-
mately become fixed in a species. For the purpose of this 
Review, we consider the effects of mobile element inser-
tions on disease as structural variants, that is, the effect 
of the presence versus the absence of the TE. Insertion 
alleles can vary from one another by polyA tail length 
or nucleotide substitutions within the TE sequence, but 
polymorphic elements reflect recent sequence insertions

retrotransposon; currently,

(L1Hs) are active.

(TPRT). The form of

elements; in humans, this 
requires L1 ORF2p-​encoded 
endonuclease and reverse

made of short interspersed

sequences; also uses L1 
protein to transpose.

inserted into the genome.

recently active in humans; also 
known as LTR elements for

Fig. 1 | Transposable elements. a | A schematic of common human 
transposable elements with their full-​length size denoted. Long interspersed 
element 1 (LINE-1 or L1) encodes two open reading frames (ORFs). ORF2p 
protein has endonuclease (EN) and reverse transcriptase (RT) domains. Alu 
elements are bipartite, with the two arms derived from 7SL RNA separated by 
an A-​rich region. SVA is a composite element containing variable number 
tandem repeats (VNTRs). Human endogenous retroviruses (HERVs) are flanked 
by long terminal repeats (LTRs) and encode three essential viral proteins, 
including envelope (Env). ERVs also exist in the genome as solo LTRs. The pie 
chart shows the proportion of the human genome made up of these repetitive 
sequences. b | L1 life cycle. L1 RNA (red) is transcribed and exported to the 
cytoplasm where it is translated into ORF1p and ORF2p (brown). The RNA 
interacts with the ORF proteins, forming a ribonucleoprotein (RNP). In the 
nucleus, the ORF2p EN domain catalyses a nick in the DNA , and RT reverse 
transcribes the L1, allowing for its insertion into the genome by target primed 
reverse transcription (TPRT). SINE, short interspersed element. pA , poly(A).
www.nature.com/nrg
Reviews
762 | DECEMBER 2019 | volume 20	
that have not had time to accumulate many substitutions. 
L1, SVA and Alu polymorphisms are present as bimodal 
alleles, with both the pre-insertion allele with no TE pre­
sent and the TE-containing allele segregating in popu­
lations19. By contrast, ERV polymorphisms are trimodal 
with pre-​insertion alleles, full-​length proviral insertion 
alleles (~10 kb) and collapsed solo LTR alleles (~1 kb) 
present in human populations42,44. Overall, polymorphic 
TEs are highly prevalent. There are more than 16,000 poly­
morphic TEs, accounting for ~24% of known structural 
variants; many of these are common variants, with over 
6,500 (36%) having a minor allele frequency >0.01 (ref.19). 
The potential for these common variants to alter complex 
disease risk is only just beginning to be appreciated, as we 
highlight in the following sections using several examples.
TE expression in disease
Both polymorphic alleles and fixed insertions can be 
autonomously transcribed. Expression of the so-​called 
‘unit’ or ‘authentic’ transcript from a TE locus47,48 — 
initiated from the TE promoter and encompassing prim­
arily its sequence — is tightly regulated as a guard against 
retrotransposition and subsequent disruption of the 
genome, which could lead to disease. Most elements are 
silenced in normal differentiated somatic cells by hetero­
chromatinization and DNA methylation49–54. Thus, 
TEs are most likely to be expressed as part of transcrip-
tional ‘read­through’ in the form of intronic sequences 
and lncRNAs48. Because of their high copy number in the 
genome, definitively associating TE-​encoded RNAs with 
specific loci is challenging using short-​read sequencing, 
and it is a common pitfall to assume that expression of a 
TE subfamily reflects genome-​wide transcription of retro­
transposition intermediates (Box 2). When TE-encoded 
promoters truly escape silencing mechanisms, they can 
produce RNAs analogous to retrotransposition inter­
mediates, TE-​encoded proteins and chimeric transcripts 
that splice into nearby genes46. TE-derived promoters 
can also regulate genes and initiate lncRNAs55.
Patterns of altered TE expression and the occur-
rence of somatic retrotransposition are incompletely 
described in many human diseases and will be important 
for understanding how TE biology affects autoimmune 
and inflammatory disorders56, neurological disease57 and 
normal ageing58. Deregulation of TEs is a hallmark of 
many malignancies. Numerous cancers, including lung, 
colon, pancreatic and ovarian cancers, have been asso-
ciated with L1 promoter hypomethylation59, L1 ORF1p 
protein expression60 (Fig. 2) and somatic L1 retrotrans-
position16,61–67. Here, acquired L1 insertions can con-
tribute cancer-​driving mutations, the best examples of 
which occur in the APC tumour suppressor gene in early 
colon cancers59,68, although such instances are uncom-
mon. ERV promoters activated in cancers can produce 
oncogenic transcripts46,69. Intriguingly, demethylating 
drugs can lead to the expression of TE-​encoded RNAs, 
and these have been associated with ‘viral mimicry’ in 
cancer cells, triggering double-​stranded RNA-​sensing 
interferon responses70–72. Evaluating whether this phe-
nomenon can be harnessed to enhance immunothera-
pies73 or induce synthetic lethalities, such as dependence 
on the adenosine deaminase acting on RNA 1 (ADAR1) 
double-​stranded RNA A-​to-​I editing enzyme74, will be 
important areas of future investigation.
Mechanisms of disease-​causing TE insertions
TE insertions can disrupt normal gene function. TE 
insertions can be highly deleterious. Here, we consider 
different mechanisms by which new insertions have 
led to monogenic disease. For additional examples of 
Colon cancer
Normal
Fig. 2 | Long interspersed element 1 expression. The image shows histologic sections 
of a colon cancer and adjacent, normal colonic epithelium. The brown stain marks 
immunoreactivity for long interspersed element 1 (LINE-1 or L1) ORF1p protein;  
cell nuclei are counterstained in haematoxylin (blue). ORF1p is selectively expressed  
in the malignant compartment.
Polymorphic TEs
Also known as retrotransposon 
insertion polymorphisms or 
polymorphic mobile element 
insertions. A transposable 
element (TE) insertion that is a 
structural variant in a 
population, present or absence 
at a locus.
Box 2 | Measuring transposable element expression
Transposable element (TE) sequences accrue neutral (non-​functional) base-​pair 
substitutions over evolutionary time. Thus, older insertions have numerous distinguishing 
(that is, locus-​specific) sequences that can be used to map TE-​encoded RNAs to specific 
genomic locations. Relatively younger TE insertions present two technical challenges146. 
First, they are more similar to one another — they have fewer distinguishing nucleotide 
sequences at each instance in the genome. Second, those TEs that are currently or 
recently active in a species will generate polymorphic insertions in populations. Many  
of these variants will not be incorporated in the reference genome assembly for the 
species, and so RNA from these loci will be misattributed to other sites. The literature 
includes many studies that interrogate TE expression at the RNA level as an investigator 
would approach a unique gene locus — using methods that rely on the hybridizations of 
PCR primers or probes (for example, reverse transcription PCR experiments, RNAse 
protection assays or in situ hybridizations), or by aggregating short-​read RNA alignments 
to consensus sequences for TE subfamilies147. All detect the TE content in RNA but, at 
least for long interspersed element 1 (LINE-1 or L1) RNA, 99% of this can be attributed to 
‘readthrough’ rather than ‘authentic’ transcription intrinsic to retroelement expression48.
Investigators who have most rigorously approached the search for TEs that are 
autonomously transcribed have developed maps of TE insertions in the samples of 
interest and used long-​read sequencing to detect strings of sequence variants or 
leveraged other types of data, such as 5′ epigenetic marks or 3′ extensions of RNAs into 
downstream, unique sequences48,59,148. These studies demonstrate highly locus-​specific 
control of TE expression. Even in samples that permit L1 expression, only a small number 
of loci ‘escape’ silencing, whereas numerous, highly similar elements elsewhere in the 
genome remain effectively methylated and transcriptionally quiescent59. To fully 
understand the contribution of TEs to the transcriptome, it will be necessary to improve 
locus-​specific analytic techniques146,149.
NaTuRE REvIEws | GEnETiCS

disease-​causing insertions identified to date, we refer 
readers to a recent in-​depth review75.
In the most straightforward mechanism, TE integra-
tion into a coding exon can disrupt gene function. This 
was the case for the first reported de novo TE insertion 
leading to disease (Fig. 3a). Haemophilia A is caused by 
loss-​of-function mutations at the factor VIII (F8) locus 
on chromosome X (Online Mendelian Inheritance in 
Man (OMIM) 306700). Studying the F8 locus in a young 
boy with all the characteristic features of haemophilia

Fig. 3 | Mechanisms by which disease-​causing transposable element 
insertions disrupt normal gene function. a | Homo sapiens-​specific L1 
(L1Hs) insertion in a patient with haemophilia A interrupts a coding exon of 
factor VIII (F8). b | AluYa5 insertion in a patient with Dent disease interrupts 
an exonic splice enhancer (ESE), resulting in skipping of exon 11, which 
introduces a stop codon in exon 12. c | L1Hs insertion in a patient with 
Duchenne muscular dystrophy disrupts a splice donor (SD) site, resulting in 
skipping of exon 44 and introduction of a stop codon in exon 45. d | Alu 
insertion in a patient with neurofibromatosis type I is located 44 bp 
upstream of NF1 exon 41, leading to skipping of exon 41 and changes in the 
open reading frame. Insertions are shown in red, exons as vertical bars 
(black) and intronic sequences as horizontal lines. Arrows denote the 
orientation of the gene (left to right, sense). Zoomed-​in regions show detail 
at the insertion sites, and scales are shown. Processed mRNA from the 
normal allele (wild type (WT)) and the insertion-​containing allele are shown 
to the right of each genomic locus with exons numbered, transposable 
element (TE)-induced skipped exons shown in grey and stop signs indicating 
premature stop codons. CLCN5, chloride voltage-​gated channel 5; DMD, 
dystrophin gene; L1, long interspersed element 1 (LINE-1); NF1,

Minor allele frequency
For biallelic loci, the allele 
frequency for the second most 
common allele, as opposed to 
the major allele frequency; the 
two sum to 1 (p + q = 1).
www.nature.com/nrg
Reviews
764 | DECEMBER 2019 | volume 20	
A and no family history, investigators found a de novo 
L1 insertion interrupting protein-​coding exon 14 (ref.76). 
Since this first report, many disease-​causing de novo L1, 
Alu and SVA insertions into exons have been identified 
that lead to a premature stop codon or frameshifted 
protein, causing many different monogenic diseases 
(for examples, see refs77–79).
Alternatively, integration of a TE into an exon can 
alter usage of that exon in the mRNA. In a patient with 
Dent’s disease (OMIM 300009), an X-​linked chronic 
renal disease caused by mutations in chloride voltage-​
gated channel 5 (CLCN5), an Alu element integrated 
into CLCN5 exon 11, disrupting a highly conserved 
exonic splice enhancer sequence (Fig. 3b). This muta-
tion resulted in the skipping of exon 11 and a truncated 
protein that was missing functional domains80.
mRNA splicing can be altered by intronic TE inser-
tions as well, as such TE insertions can directly disrupt 
splice sites. In a patient with Duchenne muscular dys-
trophy (OMIM 310200), an X-​linked disease charac-
terized by progressive muscle degeneration caused by 
mutations in the dystrophin gene (DMD), a 5′-truncated 
L1 inserted at the splice donor site of DMD exon 4481. 
This insertion resulted in skipping of exon 44 and a 
non-functional dystrophin protein81 (Fig. 3c). Even TE 
integrations that occur in the middle of the intron, not 
directly disrupting splice acceptor or donor sites, can 
alter splicing. In a patient with neurofibromatosis type 1 
(OMIM 162200), an autosomal dominant disease charac­
terized by multiple non-​cancerous nerve and skin 
tumours and caused by haploinsufficiency of the neu-
rofibromin gene (NF1), an Alu element insertion into 
intron 40, 44 bp upstream of NF1 exon 41, resulted in 
skipping of exon 6 (Fig. 3d). The resultant frameshifted 
protein was missing 77 amino acids, leading to the 
neuro­fibromatosis type 1 phenotype82. Other intronic 
Alu, L1 and SVA insertions have been reported as caus-
ing disease phenotypes by altering splicing patterns 
(for examples, see refs83–85).
New insertions can have intrinsic properties that lead to 
disease. Some TEs have cryptic splice sites and, there-
fore, de novo insertions can introduce new mRNA 
splicing patterns86–88. In some cases, the TE can provide 
just one splice site. In a patient with Lynch syndrome 
(OMIM 614337), an autosomal dominant predispo-
sition to cancer due to mutations in the orthologue of 
the Saccharomyces cerevisiae post-​meiotic segregation 
increased 2 gene (PMS2), the disease was caused by an 
intronic SVA insertion (Fig. 4a). The SVA in PMS2 intron 
7 provides a splice donor; the upstream exon 7 splices to 
an intronic sequence near the SVA, and the splice donor 
site within the SVA splices to exon 8. The additional 
72 bp introduces a premature stop codon into the open 
reading frame89.
In other cases, TEs can provide a pair of splice sites, 
which results in the ‘exonization’ of a portion of the TE. 
For example, a patient with retinoblastoma (OMIM 
180200), a retinal cancer caused by mutations in the 
RB1 gene, was found to have a full-​length L1 insertion 
in RB1 intron 14 (Fig. 4b). The L1 provides non-​canonical 
splice sites, resulting in exonization of a portion of the 
L1 sequence, with or without skipping of other nearby 
exons, leading to the disease phenotype85.
Other sequences inserted by TPRT can also provide 
splice sites, become exonized and result in disease. TPRT 
can act on cellular RNAs, which results in a processed 
pseudogene insertion. For example, a 5.8-kb TMF1 
spliced transcript was inserted into cytochrome b-245 
heavy chain (CYBB) intron 1 in a patient with chronic 
granulomatous disease (OMIM 306400), an X-​linked 
immunodeficiency disease caused by mutations in 
CYBB. Here, the TMF1 pseudogene provides two splice 
sites, 117 bp of the pseudogene exonized between CYBB 
exons 1 and 2, resulting in a premature stop codon and 
disease phenotype90 (Fig. 4c).
TE insertions associated with genomic rearrangements. 
TPRT-​mediated integrations can be accompanied by 
large interstitial deletions that lead to disease phenotypes. 
For example, two cases of neurofibromatosis type 1 
have been attributed to independent SVA insertions in 
SUZ12P accompanied by 867-kb and 1-Mb deletions 
that encompassed the NF1 gene (Fig. 4d). As mutations 
in NF1 are the well-​established cause of neurofibro­
matosis, these SVA insertions coupled with large dele-
tions explain the disease presentation91. Disease-​causing 
deletions upon TRPT-​mediated insertion have also been 
reported for L1 and Alu integrations92,93.
New TE insertions can occur through mechanisms 
other than TPRT on disease alleles. Endonuclease-​
independent retrotransposition results when a reverse-​
transcribed TE is inserted into the genome at DNA 
lesions as an imperfect mechanism of repair94. These 
endonuclease-​independent retrotransposition inser-
tions lack TPRT characteristics and are found at the 
site of a deletion or rearrangement. In a patient with 
branchio-​oto-renal syndrome (OMIM 113650), an 
autosomal dominant disease characterized by branchial, 
ear and renal anomalies and caused by mutations in the 
eyes absent homologue (EYA1), a de novo L1 insertion 
mapped to a 17-kb deletion of EYA1 (Fig. 4e). This inser-
tion was associated with loss of exons 4–7 in the EYA1 
mRNA, leading to the disease phenotype95.
We have selected this small subset of known disease-​
causing insertions to demonstrate the breadth of mech-
anisms by which TE insertions result in phenotypic 
changes. There are many other examples (reviewed else-
where75,96), and it is broadly accepted that these cases rep-
resent a small portion of disease-​causing insertions. As 
more genomes are sequenced, more de novo events will 
be found. As an example, targeted sequencing of a single 
locus, NF1, in patients with neurofibromatosis identified 
18 new independent de novo disease-​causing TE inser-
tions97. Furthermore, many TE insertions are intronic 
yet lead to disease, which emphasizes the need to look 
beyond exons and single-​nucleotide polymorphisms 
(SNPs) to identify causative mutations.
TE insertions at the population level
Polymorphic TEs causing disease. Inherited polymorphic 
TEs can severely affect gene function and cause genetic 
disease. In the Japanese population, Fukuyama-​type 
congenital muscular dystrophy is an autosomal recessive

combination of two attributes,

acting on RNA 1 (ADAR1); 
relaxes strand annealing.

with cytoplasmic double-​

into a processed transcript; in 
this review, the incorporation of 
some transposable element 
sequences into a spliced 
mRNA.

www.nature.com/nrg
Reviews
766 | DECEMBER 2019 | volume 20	
disease caused by mutations in Fukutin (FKTN) (OMIM 
253800). One FKTN variant found in this population 
contains a polymorphic SVA insertion mapping to the 
3′ untranslated region (UTR)98. The SVA introduces a 
strong splice acceptor site, and translation of this altered 
transcript results in an abnormal protein that fails to 
localize to the Golgi apparatus and accumulates in cells99. 
In Japan, the insertion is responsible for 80% of disease-​
causing alleles (Fukuyama-​type congenital muscular dys-
trophy incidence ~1 in 10,000; allele frequency ~0.008). 
Similarly, a Japanese-​specific haplotype encompassing 
a nonsense point mutation in SLCO1B1 and a delete-
rious intronic L1 insertion in the nearby SLCO1B3L1 
contribute a Rotor syndrome-​causing allele100.
Another example of a TE polymorphism in a 
population-​specific haplotype is an exonic Alu insertion 
found in the Jewish population in the MAK gene that 
leads to retinitis pigmentosa 62 (RP62; OMIM 614181). 
Here, the polymorphic Alu leads to altered MAK splicing 
patterns during development, resulting in disease101. 
Screening of nearly 2,000 unrelated probands identified 
20 homozygotes for the Alu insertion allele101.
In the Filipino population, X-​linked dystonia-​
parkinsonism is a recessive movement disorder (OMIM 
314250) caused by a polymorphic SVA insertion in an 
intron of TAF1, a gene that encodes TATA-​box bind-
ing protein associated factor 1 (ref.102). The SVA causes 
intron retention, resulting in quick degradation and 
overall lower steady-​state TAF1 transcript levels103. 
Interestingly, expansion of the hexameric (CCCTCT)n 
repeat within the SVA is associated with earlier onset of 
the disease104. This finding underscores how an allelic 
series can be derived from a single TE insertion and lead 
to phenotypic variation in a population.
Effects of common polymorphic TEs. Insertion variants 
that become common alleles potentially contribute 
to common disease risk through more subtle effects 
on gene function. Here, sequences responsible can be 
roughly located by GWAS, although the causative or 
functional variant that alters disease risk is typically 
not pinpointed precisely. Rather, GWAS identify haplo­
types marked by trait-​associated SNPs that are in 
linkage disequilibrium with numerous variants, any of 
which could predispose to disease. Many polymorphic 
TEs map near GWAS signals105,106. In fact, there is an 
enrichment of the most commonly polymorphic TE 
type, Alu elements, at GWAS loci105, and frequently these 
are in strong linkage disequilibrium with trait-​associated 
SNPs105,106. Together, these observations indicate consid-
erable potential for polymorphic TEs to contribute to 
disease risk.
Several polymorphic TEs have effects on nearby 
genes, consistent with roles in disease risk. For example, 
an intronic Alu insertion variant (allele frequency 0.29) 
that maps 97 bp downstream of a CD58 exon is on a 
haplotype associated with multiple sclerosis risk105,107. 
Patients with multiple sclerosis have reduced CD58 
expression, particularly during relapse of symptoms108. 
The Alu seems to promote skipping of the nearby exon, 
potentially decreasing functional CD58 transcript107. 
Given the distance between the Alu and the affected 
exon, it is possible that heterogeneous nuclear ribo­
nucleoprotein C (hnRNP C), an RNA-​binding protein 
that prevents exonization of Alu elements, is suppressing 
incorporation of the nearby CD58 exon (Box 1). Thus, the 
Alu may be the causative variant that increases multiple 
sclerosis risk.
Another mechanism for polymorphic TEs to alter 
common disease risk may be by influencing tran-
script levels, as some are expression quantitative trait loci 
(eQTLs)19,109. For example, a polymorphic SVA (allele 
frequency 0.25) maps to an enhancer of B4GALT1, which 
encodes a glycosyltransferase that acts on IgG106. The 
presence of the SVA is associated with reduced expres-
sion of B4GALT1 (ref.106) and decreased N-​glycosylation 
of IgG, an alteration that may relate to autoimmune and 
inflammatory phenotypes. Together, these examples 
and others demonstrate that polymorphic TEs can be 
splicing quantitative trait loci or eQTL; thereby, the TE 
genotype can alter disease risk.
There are many mechanisms by which polymorphic 
TEs can alter splicing or transcript levels (Boxes 1, 3). 
Both intrinsic effects inherent to the TE sequence and 
disruptive effects caused by TEs that interrupt pre-​
existing sequences seem to be at play in a locus-​specific 
manner. Moreover, it is highly likely that there are 
unrecognized effects of polymorphic TEs on common 
disease risk. Currently, many TE insertions are uncata-
logued or imperfectly phased with surrounding haplo-
types, and many resequencing studies in patients are not 
designed to ascertain repetitive sequence vari­ants. More 
work is necessary to understand the scale and variety of 
mechanisms by which polymorphic TEs may alter dis-
ease risk. Importantly, GWAS follow-​up studies need to 
consider these structural variants in their analysis (Box 4).
Recombination between TEs can lead to disease
Most TEs in the genome are ancient, fixed insertions 
and are no longer polymorphic in human populations. 
However, given their high copy number, these TEs

Fig. 4 | Transposable elements can cause disease by introducing regulatory sequences 
or through large regional effects. a | SVA insertion in a patient with Lynch syndrome 
provides a splice donor (SD) site to intron 7 of post-​meiotic segregation increased 2 
(PMS2), and a cryptic splice acceptor (SA) site in the intron is used. The resulting mRNA 
includes a premature stop codon, leading to PMS2 protein deficiency. b | Homo sapiens-​
specific L1 (L1Hs) inserted adjacent to RB1 exon 15 in a patient retinoblastoma provides 
multiple alternative SA sites and a SD site. Numerous abnormal mRNA isoforms are 
detected with long interspersed element 1 (LINE-1 or L1) exonization; some include the 
formation of a premature stop codon. c | A processed pseudogene of TMF1 (TMF1_ps)  
is inserted into an intron of cytochrome b-245 heavy chain (CYBB) in a patient with 
granulomatous disease and produces a new exon sequence of 117 bp that introduces  
a stop codon. d | SVA insertion results in a large deletion (grey box) that includes the 
neurofibromin gene (NF1) in a patient with neurofibromatosis type I, leading to NF1 
protein deficiency. e | L1 inserted by endonuclease-​independent retrotransposition is 
accompanied by an interstitial deletion compromising the eyes absent homologue (EYA1) 
(light-​grey box); loss of exons 4–7 results in 144 amino acids missing from the resulting 
protein. f | Alu-​mediated recombination between two Alu elements fixed in the genome  
at the hypoxanthine-​guanine phosphoribosyltransferase (HPRT1) locus leads to Lesch–
Nyhan syndrome. Two copies of the HPRT1 locus (black and blue) and two recombination-​
involved Alu elements are shown. Non-​allelic recombination between the two Alu 
elements (red) results in unequal crossover, a genomic duplication and a transcript with 
duplications of exons 2 and 3. mRNA species are shown for wild type (WT) and the patient 
with the Alu-​mediated recombination event. Loci shown as in Fig. 3. TE, transposable 
element; TRPT, target primed reverse transcription.

NaTuRE REvIEws | GEnETiCS
Reviews
	
 volume 20 | DECEMBER 2019 | 767
frequently serve as substrates for non-​allelic homo­
logous recombination, resulting in deletions and other 
rearrangements. Alu–Alu recombination events have 
led to more than 70 instances of monogenic disease110 
and can be recurrent mechanisms for some diseases. 
For example, in hereditary colorectal cancer syndrome 
familial adenomatous polyposis (OMIM 175100), which 
is caused by germline APC mutations, one study found 
>10% (4/30) of these mutations involved Alu-​mediated 
recombination; these recombinations resulted in 
deletion of coding exons or altered APC splicing111.
Whereas genomic deletions resulting from Alu–Alu 
recombination have been reported in several mono-
genic diseases112,113, Alu–Alu recombination can also 
result in duplications. Lesch–Nyhan syndrome, an 
X-​linked disease characterized by mental retardation 
and involuntary muscle movements (OMIM 300322), is 
caused by mutations in hypoxanthine-​guanine phosphor-
ibosyltransferase (HPRT). Several patients with Lesch–
Nyhan syndrome have duplicated portions of the HPRT 
locus, which arise from Alu-​mediated unequal homol-
ogous recombination and cannot produce functional 
transcripts114 (Fig. 4f). More broadly, Alu–Alu junctions 
are frequently found at boundaries of segmental dupli-
cations and more complex genomic rearrangements13,115. 
In some instances, the Alu–Alu recombination was likely 
the inciting event, although for others it has been pro-
posed that Alu sequences allowed for microhomology-​
directed DNA repair after another insult115. Similarly, 
Alu–Alu junctions have been reported at breakpoints 
following germline chromothripsis events116.
Although Alu–Alu recombination events are much 
more frequent, recombination between two L1 ele-
ments117,118 or two ERVs119 can also lead to disease. 
L1–L1 recombination was responsible for Alport syn-
drome and diffuse leiomyomatosis, a contiguous gene-​
deletion (collagen type 4 a5 (COL4A5) and COL4A6) 
syndrome resulting in associated renal disease and 
smooth muscle tumours (OMIM 308940). The patient 
had a deletion >40 kb affecting both causative genes, 
COL4A5 and COL4A6. This deletion resulted from 
unequal homologous recombination between the two L1 
loci120. Recombination between two ERV insertions has 
been identified in patients with male sterility (OMIM 
400042), where an ~800-kb region of azoospermia factor 
a (AZFa) was deleted119.
Box 3 | Additional mechanisms by which Alu may alter the phenotype
The prevalence and distinct secondary structures of Alu RNAs  
can alter gene regulation in unique ways. The Alu content may 
similarly affect long non-​coding RNA (lncRNA) metabolism and 
function. Although these mechanisms have yet to be related to 
specific disease-​causing alleles, these effects of Alu elements may  
be important.
Base-​pairing of two Alu elements oriented as inverted repeats results in 
marked secondary structures within the RNA. This pairing of Alu elements 
can affect mRNA splicing by resulting in the formation of circular RNAs 
and altered splicing patterns150 (see the figure, red arrows/Alu). Double-​
stranded RNA of paired, inverted Alu repeats can also recruit adenosine 
deaminases that act on RNA (ADAR; yellow protein in the figure), which 
edit adenosine residues to inosine151–153 (see the figure, green arrows/Alu). 
This modification is common in 3′ untranslated regions (UTRs). Although 
the functional consequence of this change is not well understood,  
it may result in nuclear retention of the RNA and loss of protein-​coding 
potential152.
Single (unpaired) Alu elements are prevalent components of 3′ UTRs, 
where they may regulate transcript levels. An Alu element in the 3′ UTR 
RNA (see the figure, blue arrows/Alu) can pair with another Alu-​containing 
transcript (pink), often a component of a lncRNA. Staufen 1 (STAU1) binds 
this double-​stranded RNA, resulting in STAU1-mediated mRNA decay154. 
Alu elements also serve as binding sites for microRNAs (miRNAs), which 
can result in downregulation of transcript levels155. Interestingly, although 
rare, Alu elements can also serve as a source of miRNA, such as miR-1285-1, 
which recognizes other Alu elements in the 3′ UTR of genes such as EIF2S3 
and CHST6, leading to their downregulation155.
De novo insertions or polymorphic Alu may contribute to disease risk by 
these mechanisms, which so far are only attributed to evolutionarily older 
Alu elements fixed in the human genome.

Chromothripsis
Chromosomal shattering.  
A large number of

genomic regions.
www.nature.com/nrg

768 | DECEMBER 2019 | volume 20	
Thus, TEs not only affect gene function by inser-
tion but also create homologous sequences that can be 
involved in genomic rearrangements at later, far distant 
points in time.
Conclusions
TEs are pervasive in our genome, and each of us is 
unique in the complement of mobile element-​derived 
structural variants and retrotransposition-​competent 
elements we inherit. These come with risks. Most 
straightforwardly, de novo and inherited TE inser-
tions can interrupt critical gene sequences and result in 
monogenic diseases. Although this mutational mecha­
nism has been considered uncommon, we stand to 
better estimate its frequency in the coming years as 
clinical exome-​sequencing analyses adopt pipelines to 
locate TE insertions and as more long-​read sequencing 
technologies are applied clinically.
Even apart from their effects on genes, TEs seem likely 
to play roles in diseased tissues. We now recognize that 
select ERV and L1 sequences are transcriptionally activated 
in cancers and that new somatically-​acquired L1 inser-
tions are accrued in cancer genomes. Much remains to be 
learned about how this dysregulation affects cancer cell 
biology and whether analogous changes in TE expression 
underlie other pathologies. Although there are examples of 
new insertions producing cancer-​driving insertions, most 
acquired L1 insertions in malignancies are widely distrib-
uted in the non-​coding genome, and more work will be 
needed to discern any functional effects of these.
Meanwhile, evaluating functional consequences of 
inherited mobile element insertions is perhaps a more 
GWAS trait-​associated SNP

## Schraiber2015_NatRevGenet_MethodsModels-UnravellingHuman

```
Records of population history are embedded in the 
patterns of DNA sequence variation that exist among 
present day individuals. Indeed, genetics has become 
integral in delineating the evolutionary history of pop­
ulations and species. However, reading the stories of 
population history written in DNA sequence variation 
is challenging and requires both comprehensive and 
accurate data and methods to interpret the compli­
cated interplay of forces (including mutation, changes 
in population size, nonrandom mating, admixture 
and selection) that shape extant patterns of genetic 
diversity.
It seems almost difficult to recall that, until fairly 
recently, inferences of human history were hampered 
by small sample sizes and limited amounts of genetic 
data. Despite these challenges, a coherent narrative of 
human evolutionary history has been forged over the 
past several decades, providing striking insights into 
when and where anatomically modern humans arose 
(~200,000 years ago, probably in East Africa), the 
‘out‑of‑Africa’ dispersal and consequent population 
bottleneck ~70,000 years ago, and the routes and tim­
ing of human migrations that led to colonization of all 
habitable parts of the world1. However, the development 
of massively parallel sequencing technology2 has ena­
bled genome-scale data sets to be generated at a frenetic 
pace3,4. Thus, the challenges are no longer related to a 
lack of data (although more comprehensive sampling 
of geographically diverse individuals is needed5), but 
rather to how to interpret the deluge of whole-genome 
and whole-exome sequences that have emerged and 
continue to emerge, which hold considerable promise 
in revealing more nuanced, realistic and complicated 
models of human history4,6–8.
Here, we focus on the methodological and interpretive 
issues that arise in the inference of human evolutionary 
history from large-scale sequencing data. We concentrate 
largely on methods and approaches for inferring increas­
ingly sophisticated models of human demographic 
history, and not adaptive evolution, which has been 
reviewed extensively9–13. We also emphasize the critically 
important role that quality control (QC) and exploratory 
data analyses (EDA) have in facilitating robust popula­
tion genomics inferences, comprehensively discuss exist­
ing methodologies and their limitations (TABLE 1), and 
highlight potential pitfalls that can arise when attempt­
ing to extract parameter-rich (that is, complex) models 
of human history from genome-scale sequencing data. 
Although our emphasis is on human evolutionary his­
tory, the methodological tools and issues discussed are 
broadly applicable. Indeed, many population genomics 
methods have been pioneered in the context of human 
data owing to the availability of large data sets, but as 
sequencing technology makes it increasingly possible to 
obtain high‑quality genomic data from other species, the 
issues and approaches described here extend well beyond 
the inference of human evolutionary history.
QC and EDA
The path from raw sequencing reads obtained from 
individuals to catalogues of single nucleotide variants 
(SNVs) involves many technical and analytical choices

Exploratory data analyses
(EDA). The initial stages of 
‘digging into’ a data set, usually 
by plotting low-dimensional 
summaries of the data.
Methods and models for unravelling 
human evolutionary history
Joshua G. Schraiber and Joshua M. Akey
Abstract | The genomes of contemporary humans contain considerable information about 
the history of our species. Although the general contours of human evolutionary history have 
been defined with increasing resolution throughout the past several decades, the continuing 
deluge of massively large sequencing data sets presents new opportunities and challenges 
for understanding human evolutionary history. Here, we review the signatures that 
demographic history imparts on patterns of DNA sequence variation, statistical methods 
that have been developed to leverage information contained in genome-scale data sets and 
insights gleaned from these studies. We also discuss the importance of using exploratory 
analyses to assess data quality, the strengths and limitations of commonly used population 
genomics methods, and factors that confound population genomics inferences.

Likelihoods
The probabilities of the data 
given various models and their 
parameters, thought of as 
functions of those parameters. 
The parameter values that 
maximize the probability of the 
data in each model are called 
maximum likelihood estimates.
Eigenvectors
Vectors that, when multiplied 
by a given matrix, still point 
in the same direction.
Covariance matrix
An n × n matrix describing the 
covariance between each pair 
in a sample of size n.
that may influence downstream analyses. Thus, initial 
QC and EDA are of considerable importance. Here, we 
outline some salient aspects of genotype calling, QC 
and EDA that are important for population genomics 
inferences.
There are two key issues involved in calling genetic 
variation from high-throughput sequencing data. First, 
short reads must be mapped to a reference genome; sec­
ond, those reads must be used to determine genotypes. 
Read mapping is influenced by the fact that sequenc­
ing reads containing sites that differ from the reference 
genome will, in general, be harder to map, because they do 
not exactly match anywhere in the genome (or they 
may even by chance match a non-homologous sequence 
elsewhere in the genome). Although modern mapping 
software can deal with a small number of mismatches 
per read, regions with clustered mutations are still likely 
to seem less variable than they truly are owing to the 
difficulty of mapping highly divergent reads.
Given a set of reads that map with high quality, call­
ing genotypes in haploid organisms is fairly straight­
forward: every site is either reference or alternative. 
However, in diploid organisms, such as humans, het­
erozygous sites introduce additional complexity into 
genotype calling. Accurate calling of heterozygous sites 
requires high‑coverage data to mitigate the effects of 
sequencing errors and the stochasticity inherent in sam­
pling each allele. For instance, at a site that is sequenced 
to a depth of 2×, observing 1 read supporting the refer­
ence allele and 1 read supporting the alternative allele 
may simply reflect a chance sequencing error. Moreover, 
for low-coverage sites, it is possible that one or the 
other allele is simply not sampled at all by any reads. 
However, for a site covered at 20×, observing 10 reads 
with a reference allele and 10 reads with an alterna­
tive allele would make a strong case for the site being 
truly heterozygous.
Several tools exist to call diploid genotypes from 
resequencing data. Early methods for SNV calling 
used simple, counting-based rules (often favouring a 
homozygous reference genotype), whereas most mod­
ern methods operate in a probabilistic framework by 
computing genotype likelihoods14. Genotype likelihoods 
quantify the probability of the observed data (that is, 
the reads covering a site), given every possible diploid 
genotype. Perhaps the most-commonly used soft­
ware for making hard genotype calls in a probabilistic 
framework is the Genome Analysis Toolkit (GATK)14–16. 
GATK requires several tuning parameters that can 
influence SNV calling in unpredictable ways; thus, it is 
important to keep up to date on best practices in using 
GATK. Other methods have been developed to perform 
downstream population genomics analyses using geno­
type likelihoods, which obviates the need to call a par­
ticular genotype at every site and more‑properly models 
the uncertainty inherent to inferences from short‑read 
data (for example, REF. 17).
When genotypes have been called, they are typically 
subjected to several filtering criteria to ensure that only 
the most‑accurate data are used in downstream analy­
ses. Parameters that are most‑commonly considered 
include sequencing depth, genotype and mapping qual­
ity18, and measures of allelic bias (that is, the propor­
tion of reads from each allele). Filtering is usually done 
on an individual basis, and then sites that are missing 
in a substantial fraction of individuals (for example, 
>10%) are removed from further analyses. Note that 
many population genetics statistics, such as nucleotide 
diversity, require knowledge of the total number of sites 
sequenced. Thus, it is critical to apply the same filtering 
criteria to all sites for each individual, and not just to 
those where a SNV was called. For example, there is a 
high probability that a heterozygous site with 2× cover­
age will be called as a homozygote. If a higher depth of 
coverage is used to filter SNV calls, then the same criteria 
should also be applied to all sites, so that an accurate 
estimate of the total number of sites used in the analysis 
can be obtained.
Another QC step that is particularly useful in large 
data sets is to filter sites that strongly deviate from 
Hardy–Weinberg equilibrium (HWE). Specifically, hav­
ing an excess of heterozygous individuals in a data set 
(in the extreme case, sites can appear as heterozygous 
in all individuals) is diagnostic of paralogous sequence 
variants that can result from mismapping of recently 
duplicated regions, or simply sites that exist in a genomic 
context that is hard to sequence. Although there are 
genome-scale deviations from HWE19, filtering out sites 
that show excessively strong departures from HWE will 
often result in removing only a small amount of data and 
avoiding serious errors in data analysis. Furthermore, 
maps of segmental duplications20 and other potentially 
problematic sequences can also be used as filtering 
criteria to focus on regions that are most amenable to 
accurate read mapping and genotype calling.
When a set of robust genotypes is obtained, it is 
useful to perform some simple EDA to assess overall 
data quality, understand characteristics of the data and 
identify potential unwanted sources of variability. For 
example, principal components analysis (PCA) forms 
a central pillar of EDA for population genomics data. 
In brief, PCA finds the eigenvectors of the covariance 
matrix derived from genotypes among individuals. 
These eigenvectors provide the coefficients of the lin­
ear combinations of genotypes that most‑effectively 
differentiate the various samples, without requiring 
a priori information on the classification of samples. 
Then, samples are typically plotted according to their 
loadings on the first few PCs. Ideally, samples will be 
separated according to their recent ancestry; individu­
als with more‑similar genotypes on average will clus­
ter closer in PC space, whereas more‑distantly related 
individuals will lie further apart (as discussed below). 
However, PCA can also be used to identify techni­
cal sources of variation, which might arise if samples 
are sequenced by different instruments or at multiple 
facilities, or are processed with different batches of rea­
gents3. It is particularly important to assess these fac­
tors when integrating data sets from distinct sources. 
Overall data summaries, such as the ratio of transitions 
to transversions, and individual-level metrics, such as 
the number of heterozygous sites, are also helpful in 
REVIEWS
NATURE REVIEWS | GENETICS	
 VOLUME 16 | DECEMBER 2015 | 729
© 2015 Macmillan Publishers Limited. All rights reserved
identifying systematic biases and poor‑quality data 
from particular samples, respectively. In short, mas­
sively parallel sequencing data sets are complex and 
imperfect. The goals of QC and EDA are to minimize 
the effect of genotyping errors on subsequent analyses 
and to better understand the non-biological factors that 
contribute to patterns of variation.
Intelligently leveraging genomic‑sequence data
A powerful feature of genome-scale data is that it allows 
careful choice of the genomic regions that are most 
appropriate to answer particular questions of inter­
est. For example, in analyses of demographic history, 
it is important to mitigate the confounding effects of 
natural selection, which can lead to biased inferences. 
Although an elegant solution to this issue would be to 
explicitly account for the joint effects of selection and 
demography, the methods to robustly do so are not well 
developed (but see REFS 21,22 for strategies that can 
simultaneously model selection and demography). A 
more‑straightforward approach is to restrict analyses 
to genomic regions that are least likely to be influenced 
by selection (FIG. 1). Indeed, carefully sampling puta­
tively neutral regions has been shown to significantly 
influence inferences such as sex-biased gene flow23,24 
and recent growth rates25. These studies have largely 
focused on genomic regions that are not in close prox­
imity to protein-coding regions. However, the develop­
ment of comprehensive functional genomics data sets, 
such as the Encyclopedia of DNA Elements (ENCODE) 
Project26 and the Roadmap Epigenomics Project27, has 
created powerful new opportunities for population 
genomics inferences by facilitating the identification of 
more‑carefully defined neutral regions. For instance, 
high-resolution maps of regulatory DNA defined by 
DNase I‑hypersensitive sites (DHS), chromatin immu­
noprecipitation followed by sequencing (ChIP–seq) and 
histone marks now exist26,27. Such data, when integrated 
with information about evolutionary conservation28, 
background selection29, regions that have experienced 
recent positive selection12 and sequences that show 
accelerated rates of evolution in the human lineage30 
(FIG. 1), allow putatively neutral sequences to be defined 
in ways that were previously not possible.
In addition, maps of recombination can also be 
integrated into a comprehensive sampling strategy to 
guide decisions on how best to minimize the effects of 
background selection (that is, choosing neutral regions 
that are not closely linked to functionally important 
sequences). Tools to automate the selection of puta­
tively neutral regions have been developed31, although 
at present they do not incorporate all of the potential 
features that could be leveraged and there is no con­
sensus on what specific combination of functional and 
comparative genomics features is best for delineat­
ing putatively neutral sequences. When using exome 
data, for example, the wealth of functional genomics 
phenotypes and evolutionary conservation can also 
be leveraged to focus on the variants that are least 
affected by selection (for instance, unconstrained four­
fold synonymous sites that do not overlap DHS), but 
background selection is still likely to be an issue when 
making inferences from protein-coding sequences. In 
short, a considerable amount of information now exists 
about the landscape of putatively functional sequences 
across the human genome, and this knowledge should 
be leveraged when making inferences of population 
demographic history.

Panmictic population
A group of individuals among 
whom random mating occurs.
Linkage disequilibrium
(LD). Nonrandom association 
between alleles at physically 
distinct genomic loci. Over 
time, this will be broken 
down by recombination.
Coalescence times
The times in the past when 
genomic regions shared a 
common genetic ancestor.
Isolation by distance
Genetic differentiation 
between individuals induced 
by geographic separation. 
Individuals that are closer 
geographically will be 
closer genetically.
Overfitting
By adding more parameters to 
a model, it will begin to model 
the noise in the observed data, 
rather than the true underlying 
mechanism of data generation. 
Overfit models will generalize 
poorly to new data sets.
Cross-validation error
The error in predicting the 
structure of a held-out portion 
of the data, when a model is 
trained on a subset of the 
whole data set. Minimizing 
cross-validation error is an 
effective way to choose 
parameters and 
hyperparameters.
Inferences of population structure and admixture
After a set of robust genotypes from appropriate 
genomic regions is obtained, inferences of popula­
tion structure are often performed. Understanding the 
genetic structure of sampled individuals serves two 
purposes. First, patterns of population structure are of 
direct interest in understanding human evolutionary 
history, times of population splitting, migration rates 
and patterns of mating among individuals. Second, 
many approaches for inferring parametric models of 
demographic history assume either a single, randomly 
mating population (a panmictic population) or knowledge 
of population structure. The development of software 
to effectively infer population structure using geno­
type data revolutionized population genomics, both as 
a tool for EDA and as a method for explicit hypoth­
esis testing32. Here, we discuss different methods and 
approaches for identifying population structure and 
inferring population genetics parameters governing 
such patterns. FIGURE 2 illustrates the types of inferences 
that can be made from the methods described below, 
assuming a fairly simple three-population model with 
differential migration.
Identifying populations and testing for admixture. 
PCA has long been an important tool for inferring and 
visualizing population genetic structure33. PCA does 
not require a priori knowledge of population structure 
because it acts to project an individual’s multilocus gen­
otype onto a small number of dimensions (often two 
dimensions) that maximally separate the data (FIG. 2). 
With sufficient amounts of data, even very fine-scale 
patterns of population structure can be detected34, which 
are sometimes more readily observed in lower‑ranked 
PCs than in higher‑ranked PCs35. When applying PCA 
to exome or genome sequencing data, it is important to 
prune SNVs based on patterns of linkage disequilibrium 
(LD), as PCA assumes that markers are independent. 
Furthermore, care must be taken to not over-interpret 
PCA results. Although it is possible to interpret PCA in 
terms of mean coalescence times between pairs of indi­
viduals36, several caveats must accompany inferences 
made from PCA. In populations with recent, large-scale 
shifts in demography (such as humans), the directions of 
highest variability may be counterintuitive. For example, 
François et al.37 found that, in models of range expan­
sion, the directions of highest variability were perpen­
dicular to the axis of expansion, which is contrary to 
the expectation that the direction of the highest vari­
ability should be parallel to the direction of expansion, 
as assumed by Cavalli-Sforza et al.33. Similarly, although 
it is possible that projections onto PC space may 
match geographical distributions of individuals under 
models of isolation by distance34,38, this depends crucially 
on the sampling strategy and should not be expected 
in most cases in which PCA is applied to genetic data 
(but see REF. 39 for a PCA-like approach that explicitly 
incorporates spatial information).
In addition to PCA, many approaches for popula­
tion assignment have been developed, but they are all 
largely similar to the popular software STRUCTURE32, 
in that they look for groups of individuals that share 
common underlying allele frequencies and that are 
mutually in HWE. However, the specific technical 
details vary among methods. The original implemen­
tation of STRUCTURE is Bayesian and uses Markov 
chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) to average over underly­
ing allele frequencies and assignments, and hence can 
be unreasonably slow for modern genomic data sets. 
By contrast, likelihood methods such as FRAPPE40 and 
ADMIXTURE41 are substantially faster and are recom­
mended for large data sets. fastSTRUCTURE42 allows for 
fast Bayesian inference of population structure, running 
on comparable timescales to ADMIXTURE but poten­
tially providing more information about uncertainty 
in a given runtime, because ADMIXTURE requires 
bootstrap resampling to compute standard errors, 
whereas fastSTRUCTURE provides estimates of credible 
intervals as a natural part of the inference.
A key issue when running a STRUCTURE-like analy­
sis is that the number of populations expected must be 
defined a priori (but see Structurama43 for a method that 
uses a Dirichlet process to jointly infer the number of 
populations along with the population assignments). 
This is because the likelihood of the data will always be 
improved by adding more parameters (that is, more pop­
ulations); however, those extra populations are merely 
overfitting the noise in the data. In the original publica­
tion, the researchers who developed STRUCTURE32 
used the marginal likelihood of the data with different 
numbers of populations to estimate the optimal number 
of populations. Although marginal likelihood compari­
son is theoretically well justified, it is difficult in prac­
tice to obtain stable estimates of the marginal likelihood 
from an MCMC run44. Therefore, care must be taken 
when using marginal likelihoods to infer the number of 
populations and, ideally, results of this analysis should be 
corroborated with alternative approaches. For example, 
ADMIXTURE recommends finding the number of pop­
ulations that minimizes the cross-validation error; that is, 
the error in predicting the genotypes of held-out markers 
across individuals. Raj et al.42 found that this approach 
does not work well with fastSTRUCTURE, and instead 
suggested using a marginal likelihood approximation 
combined with an estimate of the effective number of 
populations to obtain bounds on the possible number 
of populations.
In addition to identifying populations, STRUCTURE-
like analyses provide an estimate of the fraction of each 
individual’s genome that comes from each population 
(FIG. 2). In the case of STRUCTURE, specifically, the 
analysis is also able to assess the probability that an 
individual belongs to one or the other population; note 
that this is distinct from the more‑common activity of 
estimating admixture proportions.
Local ancestry deconvolution: chromosome painting. 
When an individual is identified as admixed, it can be 
helpful to identify the regions of their genome that come 
from the different source populations. For instance, 
identifying segments of a specific ancestry can be use­
ful in admixture mapping45. Moreover, by explicitly

Figure 2 | Inferring population demographic history.  A simple three‑population model with changes in population 
size and asymmetric gene flow is shown. a | Demographic model. b–f | Schematics for the output of various 
methodological tools discussed in the text are illustrated. Principal components analysis (PCA) qualitatively illustrates 
population structure and admixture of populations 2 and 3 by the spread of individuals along PC2 (part b). Treemix 
illustrates different migration rates between populations 2 and 3 (part c). The difference in arrowhead size indicates 
asymmetrical migration rates. dadi (diffusion approximations for demographic inference) shows a contour plot of the 
likelihood surface of the effective population size of populations 2 and 3, as well as profile likelihoods of effective 
size for each population (part d). STRUCTURE provides estimates of the proportion of each individual genomes from 
populations 1, 2 and 3 (part e). Chromosomal painting shows the specific tracts of sequences inherited from ancestors 
in each population (part f). Ind., individual; mi,j, migration rate from population i to population j; Ne.i, effective 
population size of population i; Pop., population.
